  + ## Human Strength and Power


    + Force = Mass âˆ™ Acceleration

    + Work = Force âˆ™ Displacementï¼ˆJoule, Jï¼‰

    + Power = Work / Time (Watt, W) = 1J/sec

    + Work = Torque âˆ™ Angular displacement

# Ch.3 Bioenergetics

explain the basic energy systems available to supply ATP during exercise;â€¢ understand lactate accumulation, metabolic acidosis, and cellular manifestations of fatigue;â€¢ identify patterns of substrate depletion and repletion during various exercise intensities;â€¢ describe the bioenergetic factors that limit exercise performance;â€¢ develop training programs that demonstrate the metabolic specificity of training;â€¢ explain the metabolic demands of and recovery from interval training, highintensity interval training, and combination training to optimize work-to-rest ratios.

  + ### Phosphagen system


    + $\mathrm{ADP} + \mathrm{Creatine Phosphate (CP)}\xleftrightarrow{\text{Creatine Kinase}}$\mathrm{ATP} + \mathrm{Creatine Phosphate (CP)}

    + provides ATP primarily for short-term, high-intensity activities

    + highly active at the start of all exercise regardless of intensity

    + Another important single-enzyme reaction that can rapidly replenish ATP is the adenylate kinase (also called myokinase) reaction  $$2\mathrm{ADP} \xleftrightarrow{\text{adenylate kinase}} \mathrm{ATP} + \mathrm{AMP}$$

    + **AMP** is a  powerful stimulant of glycolysis

    + é€™æ˜¯ near-equilibrium reactions : è¢«ç‰©è³ªæ¿ƒåº¦æ§åˆ¶å¹³è¡¡çš„æ–¹å‘

  + ### Glycolysis


    + åˆ†è§£ **glucose/glycogen â†’ pyruvate**

    + pyruvate ç¹¼çºŒproceedï¼šå…©å€‹æ–¹å‘


      + â†’ **Lactateï¼ˆä¹³é…¸ï¼‰** = **Fast Glycolysis**ï¼ˆå¿«é€Ÿä½†é™æ™‚ï¼‰â†’ ç„¡æ°§ä»£è¬

    + #### Lactate


      + {{< logseq/orgNOTE >}}Lactate , not lactic acid is the end product.  (lactate dehydrogenase)

  + Aerobic endurance training has been shown to reduce anaerobic energy production capabilities in rats

# Ch.4 Endocrine Responses to Resistance Exercise

understand basic concepts of endocrinology, including what hormones are and how they interact with each other and target tissues;â€¢ explain the physiological roles of anabolic hormones;â€¢ describe hormonal responses to resistance exercise; andâ€¢ develop training programs that demonstrate an understanding of human endocrine responses.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 81
hl-color:: blue


  + **General Adaptation Syndrome**: how the adrenal gland responds to a noxious stimulus (stressor)  
initial alarm reaction that includes a reduction of function -> increase in resistance to the stress above the previous baseline function. (adaptation)

  + > The specific force produced in activated fibers stimulates receptor and membrane sensitivities to anabolic factors, including hormones, which lead to muscle growth and strength changes.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 88

  + ## Heavy Resistance Exercise and Hormonal Increases

# Ch.5 Adaptations to Anaerobic Training Programs

differentiate between aerobic training adaptations and the anatomical, physiological, and performance adaptations following anaerobic training;â€¢ discuss the central and peripheral neural adaptations to anaerobic training;â€¢ understand how manipulating the acute training variables of a periodized program can alter bone, muscle, and connective tissue;â€¢ explain the acute and chronic effects of anaerobic training on the endocrine system;â€¢ elucidate the acute and chronic effects of anaerobic training on the cardiovascular system;â€¢ recognize the causes, signs, symptoms, and effects of anaerobic overtraining and detraining; andâ€¢ discuss how anaerobic training programs have the potential to enhance muscular strength, muscular endurance, power, flexibility, and motor performance.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 103
hl-color:: blue


  + <span class="r">Anaerobic training â†’ â†‘ left ventricular wall thickness, not chamber size (which is aerobic).</span>

  + ## Overview


    + Anaerobic training: high-intensity, intermittent (e.g., resistance, plyometrics, sprinting)

    + èƒ½æºç³»çµ±ï¼šPhosphagenï¼ˆ<10sï¼‰èˆ‡Glycolyticï¼ˆ20â€“60sï¼‰ç³»çµ±ç‚ºä¸»

    + é©æ‡‰åŒ…æ‹¬ï¼šâ†‘ strength, power, hypertrophy, endurance, coordination, neural & hormonal changes

  + ## Neural Adaptations


    + **â†‘ neural drive**: â†‘ agonist recruitment, firing rate, synchronization

    + **â†“ inhibition** (Golgi tendon organs)

    + **Central adaptations**: â†‘ motor cortex activity

    + **Motor unit**: â†‘ fast-twitch recruitment, selective recruitment under high load


      + size principle: é€šå¸¸å…ˆå¾µæ‹› type I, ä¸è¡Œæ‰å»æ‰¾ type II
[:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 107
hl-color:: yellow


      + **selective recruitment** è¨“ç·´å¾Œå¯å„ªå…ˆå¾µæ‹› high-threshold motor units
> With heavy resistance training, all muscle fibers get larger (i.e., hypertrophy) because motor units are recruited in a sequential order by their size to produce high levels of force. In advanced lifters, the central nervous system may adapt by allowing well-trained athletes to recruit some motor units in a nonconsecutive order, by recruiting larger ones first to promote greater production of power or speed in a movement.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 107
hl-color:: yellow


    + **NMJ**: â†‘ area, ACh receptor density


      + NMJ was found to increase in its total area.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 108
hl-color:: red


    + **Reflex potentiation**: â†‘ stretch reflex response

    + EMG: the onset of hypertrophy is associated with a decline in EMG activity
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 109
hl-color:: yellow



      + cross-education: åªç·´ä¸€é‚Š å¦å¤–ä¸€é‚Šstrength and neural activity ä¹Ÿæœƒè®Šå¼·

      + æ²’è¨“ç·´ group: bilateral deficit : The force produced when both limbs contract together is lower than the sum of the forces they produce when contracting unilaterally

      + > reduced antagonist cocontraction following resistance training, resulting in an increase in net force without an increase in agonist motor unit recruitment
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 109
hl-color:: yellow


  + ## Muscular Adaptations


    + **Hypertrophy**: â†‘ CSA of Type I & II (esp. II), 6â€“10 wk typical onset
> The process of hypertrophy involves both an increase in the synthesis of the contractile proteins actin and myosin within the myofibril and an increase in the number of myofibrils within the muscle fiber itself. The new myofilaments are added to the external layers of the myofibril, resulting in an increase in its diameter
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 110
hl-color:: yellow


    + **Fiber type shift**: IIx â†’ IIa (â†‘ fatigue resistance)

    + **Architecture**: â†‘ pennation angle

    + **Enzymes**: â†‘ creatine phosphokinase, myokinase, PFK

    + **Substrate**: â†‘ resting ATP, CP,

  + ## Connective Tissue Adaptations


    + **Bone**: â†‘ BMD via minimal essential strain (MES = ~1/10 of fracture force)


      + Select multijoint, structural exercises that involve many muscle groups at once. Avoid isolated, single-joint movements.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 115
hl-color:: yellow


      + Select exercises that direct axial force vectors through the spine and hip and apply heavier loads than single-joint assistance exercises.â€¢ Use the principle of progressive overload to stress the musculoskeletal system, and continue to progressively increase load as the tissues become accustomed to the stimulus.â€¢ Use both heavy-load exercises and ballistic or high-impact exercises to expose the bone to different intensities of force.â€¢ By varying exercise selection, it is possible to change the distribution of force insults and present a unique stimulus for new bone formation
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 115
hl-color:: yellow


    + **Tendon/Ligament**: â†‘ collagen fibril packing, strength, diameter


      + tendon stiffness (force transmission per unit of strain, or tendon elongation) increases as a result of resistance training

      + progressive high-intensity loading patterns using external resistances

      + Forces should be exerted throughout the full range of motion of a joint, and wherever possible multiple-joint exercises should be used
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 117
hl-color:: yellow


    + **Cartilage**: â†‘ thickness via moderate loading (not excessive)


      + Moderate-intensity anaerobic exercise seems to be adequate
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 117
hl-color:: yellow


      + Strenuous exercise does not appear to cause any degenerative joint disease when progressively overloaded appropriately
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 117
hl-color:: yellow


  + ## Endocrine Adaptations


    + **Acute hormonal response**: â†‘ testosterone, GH, IGF-1, cortisol

    + **Chronic changes**: resting hormones often unchanged

    + **Receptor content**: may â†“  transiently post-exercise (e.g., androgen receptor) but 
Upregulated within 48â€“72h

    + **Training prescription**: high volume + short rest â†’ â†‘ anabolic hormone spikes

  + ## Cardiovascular and Respiratory Adaptations


    + **Acute**: â†‘ HR, SV, CO, BP

    + **Chronic**: â†‘ left ventricle wall thickness (not volume), improved heart strength


      + both systolic and diastolic blood pressure decreased by 2% to 4% as an adaptation to resistance training
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 120
hl-color:: red


      + rateâ€“pressure product (heart rate î€³ systolic blood pressure; a measure of myocardial work) has been shown to either remain constant or decrease following resistance training
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 120
hl-color:: yellow


    + **Ventilatory response**: modest â†‘, not as significant as in aerobic training

  + ## Compatibility of Aerobic and Anaerobic Modes of Training


    + simultaneous sprint and aerobic endurance training decreased sprint speed and jump power
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 121
hl-color:: yellow


    + no adverse effects on aerobic power resulting from heavy resistance exercise
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 121
hl-color:: yellow


    + heavy resistance training has very limited, if any, negative effects on aerobic power (197) but instead can serve to actually enhance performance in endurance sports
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 121
hl-color:: yellow

| Strength | â†‘ 10â€“40% (depends on training status) |
| Power | â†‘ (optimal load: 30â€“60% 1RM) |
| Endurance | â†‘ oxidative capacity, buffering |
| Body comp | â†“ fat, â†‘ lean mass |
| Flexibility | â†‘ when combined with stretching |
| VO2max | â†‘ 5â€“8% in untrained |
| Motor skills | â†‘ sprint speed, jump, throwing, etc. |

  + ## Overtraining


    + Overreaching (çŸ­æœŸ)	ï¼šçŸ­æš«æ€§ â†“ è¡¨ç¾ï¼Œç¶“é©ç•¶æ¢å¾©å¾Œ â†’ è¶…é‡æ¢å¾© (â†‘)


      + functional overreaching (FOR)

      + nonfunctional overreaching (NFOR)ï¼šstagnation and a decrease in performance that will continue for several weeks or months

    + Overtraining Syndrome (OTS)	é•·æœŸæ¢å¾©ä¸å…¨ â†’ è¡¨ç¾é•·æœŸ â†“ï¼Œéœ€æ•¸é€±è‡³æ•¸æœˆæ¢å¾©

burnout, chronic overwork, staleness, unexplained underperformance syndrome, and overfatigue

      + Sympatheticï¼š	å¸¸è¦‹æ–¼é€Ÿåº¦/åŠ›é‡å‹é¸æ‰‹ï¼Œresting æ™‚ HR â†‘ã€BP â†‘ã€ç„¦æ…®â†‘

  + ## Detraining


    + **Detraining**: â†“ neural activation, CSA, Type II fiber size, ATP-CP stores

  + ### High Yield / Take-Home Points


    + **Neural adaptations precede hypertrophy**

    + **Type IIx â†’ IIa** fiber transition enhances fatigue resistance

    + **â†‘ buffering capacity** from glycolytic stress = â†‘ muscular endurance

    + **MES principle** governs bone adaptations

    + **Short rest + high volume â†’ greater hormonal spike (GH, testosterone)**

    + **Connective tissue adapts slower, needs progressive overload**

    + **Concurrent aerobic + anaerobic training may hinder power development**

# Ch.6 Adaptations to Aerobic Endurance Training Programs

identify and describe the acute responses of the cardiovascular and respiratory systems to aerobic exercise;â€¢ identify and describe the impact of chronic aerobic endurance training on the physiological characteristics of the cardiovascular, respiratory, nervous, muscular, bone and connective tissue, and endocrine systems;â€¢ recognize the interaction between aerobic endurance training and optimizing physiological responses of all body systems;â€¢ identify and describe external factors that influence adaptations to acute and chronic aerobic exercise including altitude, sex, blood doping, and detraining; andâ€¢ recognize the causes, signs, symptoms, and effects of overtraining.

      + fear of the consequences of failure

  + ## Influence of Arousal and Anxiety on Performance ğŸ¯

    + 1ï¸âƒ£  Hull's **Drive Theory**


      + arousal â†‘ â†’ è¡¨ç¾ â†‘ï¼ˆé©ç”¨ç†Ÿç·´å‹•ä½œï¼‰

      + ä¸ç†Ÿç·´è€…ï¼šå–šé†’â†‘ â†’ éŒ¯èª¤æ”¾å¤§

    + 2ï¸âƒ£ **Inverted-U Hypothesis**


      + æœ‰ä¸€å€‹æœ€ä½³å–šé†’é»ï¼ˆmoderate arousalï¼‰

      + éé«˜/éä½å–šé†’ â†’ performance â†“

    + 3ï¸âƒ£ **Individual Zones of Optimal Functioning (IZOF)**


      + æ¯äººæœ€ä½³å–šé†’å€ä¸åŒ

      + not only arousal, emotions differs too
æœ‰äººè¡¨ç¾ä½³æ–¼é«˜ç„¦æ…®ï¼Œæœ‰äººå‰‡éœ€ä½ç„¦æ…®

    + 4ï¸âƒ£ **Catastrophe Theory**


      + å–šé†’ + é«˜ cognitive anxiety â†’ performance æ€¥åŠ‡å´©æ½°

      + é”è‡¨ç•Œé»å¾Œï¼Œä¸æ˜¯ã€Œæ›²ç·šä¸‹é™ã€ï¼Œè€Œæ˜¯ã€Œå´©è§£ã€

      + å“ªä¸€ç¨® arousal å¾ˆé‡è¦
> the arousal constructs need to be more clearly delineated as cognitive anxiety, physiological arousal, somatic anxiety, or some combination of these.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 177
hl-color:: yellow


    + {{< logseq/mark >}}5ï¸âƒ£ **Reversal Theory**{{< / logseq/mark >}}

      + ä¸æ˜¯ arousal æœ¬èº«ï¼Œè€Œæ˜¯ **è©®é‡‹æƒ…ç·’çš„æ–¹å¼**
ä¾‹ï¼šå°‡ç·Šå¼µè¦–ç‚ºèˆˆå¥® â†’ performance â†‘ï¼›è¦–ç‚ºå¨è„… â†’ â†“
> athletes have within their power the ability to reverse their interpretation of their own arousal; instead of perceiving high arousal as scary and worrisome, they can choose to reverse their perception and interpret the arousal as reflecting excitement and anticipation. 
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 177
hl-color:: red


    + Skill level: An athleteâ€™s skill level can increase the latitude of optimal arousal; that is, the more skill an athlete has developed, the better he or she can perform during states of less-than or greater-than-optimal arousal
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 175
hl-color:: yellow

Task complexity: more complex moves -> needs less arousal

  + ### Self-Efficacy


    + **Self-confidence** : belief that one can successfully perform a desired behavior
**Self-efficacy** :  situationally specific form of self-confidence
{{< logseq/mark >}}the perception of oneâ€™s ability to perform a given task in a specific situation{{< / logseq/mark >}}

    + better predictor of task execution than either arousal or anxiety

    + Bandura's theory: é€™å¹¾é …çµ„æˆå› ç´ 


      + Performance accomplishmentsâ€”past experiences of success or failure

      + Vicarious experiencesâ€”watching others (modeling)

      + Verbal persuasionâ€”encouragement from self or others

      + Imaginal experienceâ€”using imagery to see oneself perform

      + Physiological statesâ€”perception of arousal as facilitative or debilitative

      + Emotional statesâ€”affect or mood

    + > Self-efficacy influences peopleâ€™s choice of activity, their level of effort in that activity, and how much persistence they will have in the face of challenging obstacles.

hl-page:: 183
hl-color:: yellow

  + ### Goal Setting ğŸ¯

**Pursuing progressively challenging tasks with defined performance criteria **

    + Goals direct an athleteâ€™s attention by prioritizing efforts.
Goals increase effort because of the contingency of success on goal attainment.
Goals increase positive reinforcement through the feedback given to athletes.

    + ç¨®é¡ï¼š

      + **Process**ï¼ˆæŠ€è¡“åŸ·è¡Œï¼Œå¦‚æ“ºè‡‚è§’åº¦ï¼‰

      + **Outcome**ï¼ˆçµæœï¼šè´çƒã€ç ´ç´€éŒ„ï¼‰

      + **Short-Term Goals** directly related to current training or competition and are guidelines that can be attained in a relatively short time frame

      + **Long-term goals** are those that overarch the series of linked short-term goals.

    + âœ… Process goals æœ€èƒ½ä¿ƒé€²å‹•ä½œæ”¹é€²èˆ‡è‡ªæˆ‘æ•ˆèƒ½â†‘

  + ## Nutrition Guideline


    + Myplate is starting point

      + **æ²¹è„‚ä¸åˆ—å…¥äº”å¤§é¡**ï¼Œä½†å« EFAã€Vit Eï¼Œäº¦å»ºè­°æ¯æ—¥æ”å–é‡

# Ch.10 Nutrition Strategies for Maximizing Performance

list pre-, during- and postcompetition nutrition recommendations for different sports;â€¢ provide guidelines for weight gain and weight loss;â€¢ recognize signs and symptoms of eating disorders;â€¢ understand the importance of having an intervention and referral system in place for athletes suspected of having an eating disorder;â€¢ recognize the prevalence and etiologies of obesity; andâ€¢ assist in the assessment process for obese individuals

    + ### Carbohydrate Loading


      + commonly used carbohydrate loading regimen includes three days of a high-carbohydrate diet in concert with tapering exercise the week before competition and complete rest the day before the event.

# Ch.11 Performance-Enhancing Substances and Methods

provide reliable and up-to-date information to athletes on the risks and benefits of performance-enhancing substances, including anabolic steroids;â€¢ understand the efficacy and adverse effects of over-the-counter dietary supplements marketed to athletes for enhancing sport and exercise performance;â€¢ determine which performance-enhancing supplements are beneficial for strength/ power performance, endurance performance, or both; andâ€¢ distinguish between those performanceenhancing supplements that mimic the effects of hormones in the body and those that improve performance through some other means

  + The distinction between a drug and a dietary supplement is linked to FDA approval for safety and effectiveness.

    + ### ğŸ”¹ b-Hydroxy-b-methylbutyrate (HMB)
 derivative of the EAA leucine and its metabolite a-ketoisocaproic acid.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 255
hl-color:: yellow

å¢åŠ è›‹ç™½è³ªåˆæˆ æ¸›å°‘åˆ†è§£ by ubiquitin. åˆæ³•.
> ((684cf602-7ade-4379-8c23-94124d8d7209))

      + Wilson GJ, Wilson JM, Manninen AH. Effects of beta-hydroxy-beta-methylbutyrate (HMB) on exercise performance and body composition across varying levels of age, sex, and training experience: A review. Nutr Metab (Lond). 2008 Jan 3;5:1. doi: 10.1186/1743-7075-5-1. PMID: 18173841; PMCID: PMC2245953.

> While various studies analyzed in this manuscript support the efficacy of HMB as an effective ergogenic aid for athletes that **decreases DOMS, markers of muscle damage, and body fat, while increasing various markers of performance, including LBM and strength in resistance trained athletes, and OBLA and VO2 peak in endurance trained athletes**, a number of studies analyzed did not support the efficacy of HMB supplementation.

> There is compelling evidence that HMB supplementation may be useful for clinical muscle wasting conditions including AIDS, cancer, bed-rest, and during periods of caloric deficits. HMB also appears to be safe, and may improve various markers of health, including blood pressure and LDL-cholesterol. When supplementing with HMB, current evidence suggests that 1 g of HMB should be consumed 3 times per day, for a total of 3 g of HMB daily (or 38 mg/kg of bodyweight). However, more studies are needed to determine the optimal dosage and frequency of HMB supplementation, and the overall efficacy of HMB supplementation as an ergogenic aid for athletes.

      +

      + è‚Œè‚‰ç·©è¡èƒ½åŠ›ï¼ˆMBC, muscle buffering capacityï¼‰â†’ æŠµæŠ—é…¸åŒ–ï¼Œâ†‘é‹å‹•è¡¨ç¾

      + MBC èˆ‡ä»¥ä¸‹æœ‰æ­£ç›¸é—œï¼šrepeated sprint ability, anaerobic threshold, training volume, high-intensity capacityï¼ˆe.g. soccer, sprint, hockey, cycling, rowing, triathlonï¼‰

      + The ability to regulate H + concentration in skeletal muscle during high-intensity exercise has been termed muscle buffering capacity (MBC)

# Ch.12 Principles of Test Selection and Administration

identify and explain reasons for performing tests,â€¢ understand testing terminology to communicate clearly with athletes and colleagues,â€¢ evaluate a testâ€™s validity and reliability,â€¢ select appropriate tests, andâ€¢ administer test protocols properly and safely. The author would like to acknowledge the significant contribution of Everett Harman to this chapter.

hl-color:: blue


  + ## Reasons for Testing


    + ğŸ¯ **ç‚ºä½•é€²è¡Œæ¸¬é©—ï¼Ÿ**

> Testing can be used to assess athletic talent, identify physical abilities and areas in need of improvement, set goals, and evaluate progress

hl-color:: yellow


      + è©•ä¼°é¸æ‰‹é«”èƒ½ï¼ˆtalent identificationï¼‰

      + æ‰¾å‡ºéœ€æ”¹å–„èƒ½åŠ›ï¼ˆtraining focusï¼‰

      + è¨­å®šç›®æ¨™ï¼ˆgoal settingï¼‰

      + æª¢é©—é€²æ­¥ï¼ˆprogress trackingï¼‰

    + ğŸ’¡ Testing = è¨“ç·´è¦åŠƒçš„å®¢è§€ä¾æ“š

  + ## Testing Terminology

  + ## Evaluation of Test Qualityï¼ˆæ¸¬é©—å“è³ªè©•ä¼°ï¼‰

  + ### ğŸ” å…©å¤§æ ¸å¿ƒï¼š


    + **Validityï¼ˆæ•ˆåº¦ï¼‰** = æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦æ¸¬å‡ºå®ƒæƒ³æ¸¬çš„æ±è¥¿ï¼ˆæœ€é‡è¦ï¼‰
> Validity is the degree to which a test or test item measures what it is supposed to measure; this is one of the most important characteristics of testing.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 267
hl-color:: yellow


    + **Reliabilityï¼ˆä¿¡è³´åº¦ï¼‰** = æ¸¬é©—çµæœæ˜¯å¦ä¸€å†ä¸€è‡´

  + ### æ•ˆåº¦é¡å‹ï¼š


    + | é¡å‹ | å®šç¾© | ç¯„ä¾‹ |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| **Construct Validity** | æ¸¬é©—æ˜¯å¦çœŸæ­£åæ˜ æŸæŠ½è±¡æ¦‚å¿µï¼ˆç†è«–æ ¸å¿ƒï¼‰ | <span class="r">å‚ç›´è·³æ¸¬çˆ†ç™¼åŠ› power</span> |
| **Face Validity** | è¡¨é¢çœ‹ä¾†æ˜¯å¦åˆç†ï¼ˆä¸ç­‰åŒæ–¼ç§‘å­¸é©—è­‰ï¼‰ | æ•™ç·´è¦ºå¾— shuttle run å¾ˆåƒæ¯”è³½æƒ…å¢ƒ |
| **Content Validity** | æ¸¬é©—æ˜¯å¦æ¶µè“‹æ‰€æœ‰ç›¸é—œé¢å‘ | é«”èƒ½æ¸¬é©—æ˜¯å¦åŒ…å«åŠ›é‡ã€æœ‰æ°§ã€æ•æ·ç­‰ |
| **Criterion-Referenced Validity** | æ¸¬é©—çµæœèˆ‡å·²é©—è­‰æ¨™æº–çš„ç›¸é—œæ€§ | ä¸€å€‹æ–°æ¸¬é©—èˆ‡ VOâ‚‚max ä¹‹ç›¸é—œä¿‚æ•¸é«˜ |

    + Criterion-referenced validity

      + | é¡å‹ | å®šç¾© | é—œéµå·®ç•° | ç¯„ä¾‹ |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| **Concurrent Validity** | æ¸¬é©—çµæœæ˜¯å¦èˆ‡ç›®å‰å¯æ¥å—çš„**é‡‘æ¨™æº–æ¸¬é©—**ä¸€è‡´ | âœ… **åŒæ™‚æ¯”å°** | å‚ç›´è·³ vs. é«˜ç§‘æŠ€åŠ›æ¿åŒæ­¥æ¸¬çˆ†ç™¼åŠ› |
| **Predictive Validity** | æ¸¬é©—çµæœæ˜¯å¦èƒ½**é æ¸¬æœªä¾†è¡¨ç¾** | âœ… **æœªä¾†å°å‘** | 40-yard dash æ¸¬æˆç¸¾æ˜¯å¦èƒ½é æ¸¬ WR è¡¨ç¾ |
| **Discriminant Validity** | æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦**å€åˆ†ä¸åŒèƒ½åŠ›æˆ–æ—ç¾¤** | âœ… **èƒ½å€åˆ†æ‰æœ‰æ„ç¾©** | ä¸€é …è‚ŒåŠ›æ¸¬é©—ä¸æ‡‰èˆ‡æŸ”è»Ÿåº¦é«˜åº¦ç›¸é—œ |

      + {{< logseq/mark >}}**Concurrent validity** is the **extent** to which test scores are associated with **those of other accepted tests**{{< / logseq/mark >}} that measure the same ability

        + **Convergent validity** is evidenced by **high positive correlation between results of the test being assessed and those of the recognized measure of the construct(the â€œgold standardâ€).**

        + Convergent Validity	æ¸¬é©—èˆ‡ç†è«–ä¸Šç›¸ä¼¼çš„å…¶ä»–æ¸¬é©—çµæœä¸€è‡´æ€§	é€šå¸¸é€é**ç›¸é—œä¿‚æ•¸ï¼ˆrï¼‰**æª¢é©—ï¼Œr > 0.7 é€šå¸¸ç‚ºä½³ çœ‹æ˜¯å¦â€œèšåˆâ€åˆ°åŒæ§‹å¿µ

      + **Predictive validity** corresponds with future behavior or performance.

      + **Discriminant validity** distinguish between two different constructs and is evidenced by a low correlation between the results of the test and those of tests of a different construct

      + Concurrent = ç¾åœ¨ vs. æ¨™æº– â†’ ã€Œæˆ‘é€™å€‹æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦è·Ÿå…¬èªæº–çš„å°ä¸Šï¼Ÿã€

      + Predictive = ç¾åœ¨ vs. æœªä¾† â†’ ã€Œæˆ‘é€™æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦é æ¸¬èª°èƒ½æˆåŠŸï¼Ÿã€

      + Discriminant = ä¸è©²ç›¸é—œçš„æœç„¶ç„¡é—œ â†’ ã€Œæˆ‘é€™æ¸¬é©—èƒ½å¦åªæ¸¬æˆ‘æƒ³æ¸¬çš„æ±è¥¿ï¼Ÿã€

  + ### **Reliabilityï¼ˆä¿¡è³´åº¦ï¼‰= ç©©å®šæ€§**

    + æ¸¬é©—**æ˜¯å¦èƒ½åœ¨ä¸åŒæ™‚é–“ã€æ“ä½œè€…ã€å ´åœ°ä¸‹å¾—åˆ°ä¸€è‡´çµæœï¼Ÿ**

    + â— å¯é  â‰  æœ‰æ•ˆï¼ˆä½ å¯ä»¥**ç©©å®šæ¸¬éŒ¯èª¤çš„æ±è¥¿**ï¼‰

    + #### ğŸ“Œ Testâ€“Retest Reliability

Statistical correlation of the scores from two administrations

      + åŒä¸€æ¸¬é©—åœ¨ä¸åŒæ™‚é–“æ–½æ¸¬ â†’ çµæœæ‡‰ç›¸è¿‘

    + æ¸¬é‡ä¿¡åº¦çš„å·¥å…· **typical error of measurement (TE)**
equipment error + biological variation of athletes åŒ…å«

      + 1. **Intrasubject variability** is a lack of consistent performance by the person being tested

      + 2. **Objectivityï¼ˆå®¢è§€æ€§ï¼‰** = **Interrater reliability**

        + ä¸åŒæ¸¬é©—è€…å°åŒä¸€å‹•ä½œæ˜¯å¦çµ¦å‡ºç›¸åŒè©•åˆ†

      + 3. **Intrarater variability** is the lack of consistent scores by a given tester è‡ªå·±çµ¦åˆ†æ•¸ä¸æ¨™æº–

      + 4. Test æœ¬èº«å°±ç„¡æ³• consistent

  + ## Test Selectionï¼ˆé¸æ“‡æ¸¬é©—åŸå‰‡ï¼‰


    + ğŸ’¡æ¸¬é©—å¿…é ˆ**ç¬¦åˆæ¯”è³½ç‰¹æ€§èˆ‡é‹å‹•å“¡ç‹€æ³**

    + åŸå‰‡ï¼š


      + èƒ½é‡ç³»çµ±åŒ¹é…ï¼ˆå¦‚ 300-yard shuttle å°æ‡‰ anaerobicï¼‰

      + å‹•ä½œç‰¹ç•°æ€§ï¼ˆå¦‚å‚ç›´è·³ vs. è¸¢è…¿ï¼‰

      + æŠ€è¡“é›£åº¦èˆ‡ç¶“é©—ï¼ˆæ–°æ‰‹é¿å…æŠ€è¡“ä¾è³´é«˜çš„æ¸¬é©—ï¼‰

      + å¹´é½¡ & æ€§åˆ¥ï¼ˆå¦‚é’æ˜¥æœŸæ¸¬é©—å®‰æ’ï¼‰

      + Environmental factors such as temperature, humidity, and altitude

    + ### Aerobic Endurance Testing in the Heat


      + **Using temperature limits at least 5Â°F (3 Â°C) below those listed is recommended, especially on sunny days, to provide a safety margin and enable better test performance.**

      + acclimate at least a week from cold climate

      + hydration 24hr prior

      + Plain water is most appropriate for exercise up to 1 hour in duration.

  + ## Test Administrationï¼ˆæ¸¬é©—å®‰æ’ï¼‰


| é …ç›® | å»ºè­° |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| **æ¸¬é©—é †åº** | æŠ€è¡“/çˆ†ç™¼â†’åŠ›é‡â†’è€åŠ›ï¼ˆé¿å…ç–²å‹å½±éŸ¿ï¼‰ |
| **æ¸¬é©—æ¢ä»¶æ§åˆ¶** | åŒæ¨£æ™‚é–“ã€å ´åœ°ã€å¤©æ°£ã€æº«åº¦ã€hydration |
| **æ¸¬é©—äººå“¡** | ç†Ÿæ‚‰æµç¨‹ã€é¿å…é¼“å‹µå·®ç•°ï¼ˆå½±éŸ¿ä¿¡è³´åº¦ï¼‰ |
| **æ¸¬å‰æº–å‚™** | æ¨™æº–æš–èº«ï¼ˆgeneral + specificï¼‰ã€å…ˆç¤ºç¯„èˆ‡èªªæ˜ |
| **æ¸¬å¾Œæ¢å¾©** | é«˜å¿ƒè·³æ¸¬é©—ï¼ˆå¦‚300-yard shuttleï¼‰é ˆ cooldown |

    + ### Test sequence


      + 1. Nonfatiguing tests (e.g., height, weight, flexibility, skinfold and girth measurements, vertical jump)

      + **2. Agility tests (e.g., T-test, pro agility test)**

      + 3. Maximum power and strength tests (e.g., 1RM power clean, 1RM squat)

      + 4. Sprint tests (e.g., 40 m sprint with split times at10 m and 20 m)

      + 5. Local muscular endurance tests (e.g., push-up test)

      + 6. Fatiguing anaerobic capacity tests (e.g., 300-yard[275 m] shuttle)

      + 7. Aerobic capacity tests (e.g., 1.5-mile [2.4 km] run or Yo-Yo intermittent recovery test)

# Ch. 13 Administration, Scoring, and Interpretation of Selected Tests

  + ## Measuring Parameters of Athletic Performance

    + **Wingate æ¸¬é©—**å±¬æ–¼ laboratory æ¸¬ anaerobic power â†’ è‹¥ç¾å ´é›£åŸ·è¡Œï¼Œæ”¹ç”¨ field-testï¼ˆå¦‚ medicine ball throwï¼‰

    + Tests of **speed** usually < 100 m (109.4 yards) , longer distances reflect anaerobic or aerobic capacity more than absolute ability to move the body at maximal speed

  + ## Selected Test Protocols and Scoring Summary

  + ### Strength

    + #### 1RM Bench Press
type:: test
category:: strength
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Olympic barbell, flat bench, weight plates, collars, spotter arms or racks

      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1â€“2 trained spotters, 1 test administrator

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. General warm-up + specific warm-upï¼ˆ5â€“10 reps with light loadï¼‰  
2. Rest 1 min â†’ 3â€“5 reps @ ~60â€“70% est. 1RM  
3. Rest 2 min â†’ 2â€“3 reps @ ~80% 1RM  
4. Rest 2â€“4 min â†’ attempt 1RM  
5. Increase weight 5â€“10% per attempt until failure  
6. Full ROM: bar touches chestï¼ˆno bounceï¼‰, full elbow lockout

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:

        + Male collegiate average: ~1.05Ã—BWï¼ˆgoodï¼‰ï¼›Elite >1.5Ã—BW

        + Combine settings often use submax RMï¼ˆe.g. 225lb repsï¼‰ instead

    + #### 13.2 1RM Bench Pull
type:: test
alias:: 
category:: strength
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Flat bench with support for horizontal pull, barbell, lifting straps optional

      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to confirm rep standard

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Lie prone on bench, barbell racked under chest level  
2. Pull bar upward to touch underside of bench without torso motion  
3. Lower under control, full extension  
4. Use same warm-up & 1RM progression as bench press

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:

        + Torso must stay in contact with bench at all times é ­ä¹Ÿæ˜¯
Overextension = invalid rep
Should not allow excessive elbow flare

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:

        + Often paired with 1RM Bench Press to assess push-pull ratio
Ideal Push:Pull ratio â‰ˆ 1:1 in balanced athletes

    + #### 13.3 1RM Back Squat
type:: test
category:: strength
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Olympic barbell, squat rack or power cage, safety pins

      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 2 trained spotters, 1 ç´€éŒ„

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Warm-up progression same as Bench Press  
2. Bar rests on upper trapsï¼ˆnot neckï¼‰  
3. Feet shoulder-width, toes slightly out  
4. Descent: thighs parallel to floor = minimum depth  
5. Ascend to full extension without bar shifting

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:

        + Monitor for valgus collapse, trunk lean, and heel lift
Full depth standard = thigh parallel
Spotters must coordinate hand placement & timing

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:

        + Male collegiate average: 1.5â€“2.0Ã—BWï¼ˆelite >2.5Ã—BWï¼‰
Excellent for force development assessment
Pair with VJ to evaluate strengthâ€“power profile

  + ### Maximum Muscular Power

    + #### 1RM Power Clean
too techy, å°‘ç”¨

      + Strong correlation with vertical jump and sprint performance

    + #### 13.5 Standing Long Jump
type:: test
category:: power
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Measuring tape (cm/in), flat jumping area at least 20 feet (6 m) in length

      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: One distance judge, one recorder

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Feet shoulder-width behind start line  
2. Arm swing + 2-foot takeoff  
**3. Land with both feet, no backward fall**  
4. Measure from start line to rear-most heel contact  
5. Best of 3 trials used to 1cm

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:  
Male college athletes average: ~2.3â€“2.5m  
High correlation with sprint acceleration and leg power
Elite PR50 250 280 PR90 315 375
[:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 316
hl-color:: yellow


    + #### 13.6 Vertical Jump
type:: test
alias:: VJ, CMJ, Countermovement Jump
category:: power
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: chalk, wall, measuring tape or Vertec
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 284
hl-color:: yellow


      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 tester/recorder

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
è·é›¢ 15cm from wall
1. Measure standing reach (1-hand flat reach)  
2. Perform countermovement (quick dip) + jump and tap highest marker  
3. Record difference between jump height and standing reach  
4. 3 attempts, best score recorded

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:  
No running or step-in allowed  
Fingertip must clearly tap marker  
Must land with control to count

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:  
Male college athlete norm:  50. women: 40-50
Can be used to calculate Peak Power via Sayers formula

    + #### 13.7 Static Vertical Jump
type:: test
alias:: Squat Jump, SJ
category:: power
tags::

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  same as VJ but no counter-movement
1. Begin in squat position (~90Â° knee angle), no countermovement  
2. Hold 2â€“3 sec, then jump vertically as high as possible  
3. Hands on hips (if required)  
4. Best of 3 jumps recorded

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:  
No arm swing or countermovement permitted  
Failure to hold preload squat = invalid rep  
High variation in beginner execution

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:  
Isolates concentric power (no stretch-shortening cycle)  
Useful in profiling SSC utilization compared to CMJ
> The ratio of the vertical jump height with countermovement to squat jump height can be calculated as the eccentric utilization ratio
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 286
hl-color:: yellow


    + #### 13.8 Reactive Strength Index
type:: test
alias:: RSI
category:: power / plyometric
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Contact mat or dual force plates + timer , boxes

      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1  with stopwatch or sensor interface

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
ç®±å­åœ°æ¯¯è·é›¢è‡³å°‘20cm
1. Perform depth (drop) jump from 20â€“60 cm box  . hands on hips.
2. Upon ground contact, immediately rebound vertically  
3. Measure flight time and contact time  
4. **RSI = jump height Ã· contact time**

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:  
Delayed rebound or pause = invalid  
Ensure consistent box height across testing  
Monitor contact mat sensitivity

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:  
Higher RSI = better stretch-shortening cycle efficiency  
Elite athletes RSI ~2.0+ (msâ»Â¹)

    + #### 13.9 Margaria-Kalamen Test
type:: test
alias:: Stair Sprint Power Test
category:: power
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Stopwatch,  >9-step stair with ~17.8 cm steps, åŠ©è·‘6m
  [:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 288
hl-color:: yellow


      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 timer  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Start 6m from base of stairs  
2. Sprint and step on 3rd, 6th, and 9th steps as fast as possible  
3. Time measured between 3rd and 9th step contact  
4. Power = (body mass (newton) Ã— vertical distance) Ã· time

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:  
Only valid if correct steps are hit cleanly  
Shoes must fully contact step  
Risk of trip if timing is off

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:  
Assesses short burst anaerobic power  
Better for football/track athletes than general populations
watts
**20**-30 exellent: **20**00, 1600 å¥³ç”Ÿexellent=ç”·ç”Ÿaverage
30-40 exellent: 1600, 1200

  + ### Anaerobic

    + #### 13.10 300-Yard Shuttle
type:: test
alias:: Anaerobic Capacity Shuttle
category:: anaerobic capacity
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Stopwatch, 25-yard marked lanes, cones

      + ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Place 2 cones 25 yards apart  
2. Sprint 25 yards out and back = 1 rep  
3. Complete 6 total reps (6 Ã— 50y = 300 yards)  
4. Time entire effort with stopwatch  
5. Perform 2 trials separated by full recoveryï¼ˆ~5 minsï¼‰  
6. **Average of both trials** is score

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Turns must be sharp, touching or crossing line
Test must be conducted on flat, non-slip surface
Fatigue may cause pacingâ€”encourage maximal effort

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Assesses anaerobic lactic capacity + repeated sprint fatigue
Often used for football, soccer, basketball athletes
Collegiate male average: 55â€“60 sec. Normal: 72

  + ### Local Muscular Endurance

    + #### 13.11 Partial Curl-Up
type:: test
alias:: Abdominal Endurance Test
category:: local muscular endurance
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Mat, masking tape (**7.6 cm** / 4.5 in spacing), metronome  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 rep counter  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Lie supine, knees at 90Â°, arms by sides, fingers to tape  
2. **Metronome at 40 bpm** controls cadence  
3. Curl up until fingertips reach second tape, then return  
4. Max reps = 75 or until failure

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Movement must be pacedâ€”no rushing or skipping beats
Pelvis/feet must remain on floor
Ensure smooth motion; no jerking

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Targets rectus abdominis + obliques endurance
Less stressful than full sit-up for lumbar spine
Valid for general population, not max effort athletes
exellent: 75 55
average: 30-40 ; 20-30

    + #### 13.12 Push-Up Test
type:: test
alias:: Upper-Body Endurance Test
category:: local muscular endurance
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Mat or flat surface  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 rep counter  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Male: standard push-up position  
 Female: modified (knees on ground)  
2. Lower body to 90Â° elbow flexion or chest contact foam block  
3. As many reps as possible without time limit  
4. Test ends when failure or break in form

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
No pause allowed at top or bottom
Hips must remain aligned (no sagging or piking)
Use same rep standard across retests

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Military and fire academies often use for screening
Exellent: Male 30â€“40; Female 25â€“30
Not highly correlated with absolute strength, more with endurance

    + #### 13.13 YMCA Bench Press Test
type:: test
category:: local muscular endurance
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Olympic bar, flat bench, metronome, spotter  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 spotter  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Male: 80 lb barbell; Female: 35 lb barbell  **36kg 16kg**
2. Metronome at 60 bpmï¼ˆ30 reps/min cadenceï¼‰  
3. Perform bench press to cadence until failure  
4. Terminate if subject canâ€™t match pace for 2 reps

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Requires rhythm coordinationâ€”not suitable for all athletes
Watch for uneven reps or bouncing
Not valid for measuring max strength

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Assesses submax endurance under standardized load
Useful in general population, PE settings
Compare reps to normative tables (YMCA standards)
yMCA Bench Press norms
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 321
hl-color:: yellow


  + ### Aerobic

    + #### 13.14 1.5-Mile (2.4 km) Run
type:: test
category:: aerobic capacity
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Measured 1.5-mile (2.4 km) flat course, stopwatch
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 safety observer if needed  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Subject runs 1.5 miles as fast as possible  
2. Time is recorded in minutes:seconds  
3. Optional: use formula to estimate VOâ‚‚max  
 VOâ‚‚max = (483 / time in minutes) + 3.5

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Heat, wind, elevation affect performance
Medical clearance may be needed for high-risk groups
Warm-up recommended; pacing strategies vary

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Best for trained runners and conditioned individuals
College male norm: ~10:30â€“12:00; female ~12:00â€“14:00
High face validity for distance/endurance sports
PR50 : VO2max 45 12:00ã€‚ 38 15:00
PR90: VO2max 54 9:34ã€‚  47 11:00

      + <span class="r">VO2 max norms</span> 
tags:: HY
[:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 324
hl-color:: yellow


    + #### 13.15 12-Minute Run (Cooper Test)
type:: test
alias:: Cooper Test
category:: aerobic capacity
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Track or treadmill, cones for markers, stopwatch  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 counter/observer  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Subject runs for 12 continuous minutes  
2. Total distance covered (meters) is recorded  
3. VOâ‚‚max estimation (meters):  
 VOâ‚‚max = (Distance in meters âˆ’ 504.9) Ã· 44.73

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Pacing is critical; early overexertion leads to drop-off
Not ideal for untrained or clinical populations
Must measure distance precisely

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Cooper test used globally for endurance assessments
Used in military, soccer, and fire academies
Male average: 2.4 ; Female: 2.1 km
PR90 2.8ã€‚ 2.5ã€‚

    + #### 13.16 Yo-Yo Intermittent Recovery Test (Level 1)
type:: test

tags::

hl-page:: 294
hl-color:: yellow


      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Cones, {{< logseq/mark >}}20m lane{{< / logseq/mark >}}, audio CD/app, measuring tape  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 observer  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Sprint 20m â†’ turn â†’ return to start within audio cue  
2. 5â€“10 sec recovery in 5m jog zone  
3. Continue until failure (2 misses = termination)  
4. Record last completed stage and distance

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Requires space, clear audio system, and athlete familiarity
Motivation & familiarity affect result consistency
Not valid if cone touches or cut corners

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Better reflects team sport conditioning than steady-state tests
High correlation with repeat sprint and VOâ‚‚max
ğŸ§ Elite male: >2000m; average trained: 1400â€“1800m

    + #### 13.17 Maximal Aerobic Speed (MAS) Test
type:: test
alias:: VÌ‡Oâ‚‚max Speed Test
category:: aerobic capacity
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: 200m+ track, cones, audio signal or pacing system  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator, 1 timekeeper  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Athlete runs at increasing speeds, guided by audio pacing  
2. Speed increases ~1 km/h every 2 minutes  
3. Continue until failure to keep pace  
4. MAS = highest speed maintained for full stage

      + V.O2 max = 3.5 x MAS (km/hr)

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Needs space and clear markers for distance
Best administered outdoors with clear pacing
Environmental factors affect max speed

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
MAS used to prescribe aerobic intervals (e.g., 120% MAS)
More precise than Cooper/Yo-Yo for programming intensity
Elite endurance MAS ~18â€“20 km/h

  + ### Agility

    + #### 13.18 T-Test
type:: test
alias:: T-Agility Drill
category:: agility
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: 4 cones (5-10-5 yd), stopwatch, tape measure  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Set cones in â€œTâ€ formationï¼š10 yd top, 5 yd stem  
2. Sprint forward 10 yd, shuffle left 5 yd, shuffle right 10 yd, shuffle left 5 yd, backpedal 10 yd  
3. Must touch **base of each cone** with hand  
4. Time recorded from start to full return

      + best time of two trials

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Hand must touch cone base or trial invalid
Footwork form affects time significantly
Lateral shuffles = no crossover steps

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Tests lateral + linear movement transitions
Male avg (collegiate): ~9.5â€“10.5 sec
Common in football, basketball, and volleyball

    + #### 13.19 Hexagon Test
type:: test
alias:: Hex Jump Test
category:: agility + coordination
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Athletic tape to mark hexagon (24 in sides), stopwatch  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 floor judge  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Form hexagon with 6 equal 24-in sides, 120Â° angles  
2. Jump over each side and back to center in clockwise direction  
3. 3 full revolutions (~18 jumps), time recorded  
4. Repeat in opposite direction (optional)

      + best time of three trials is recorded

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Only 2-foot jumps allowed
Misstep = restart or penalty
Requires spatial awareness and coordination

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Tests multidirectional reactivity and lower limb stability
Norm: Male ~12â€“13 sec; Female ~13â€“15 sec

    + #### 13.20-pro-agility-test
type:: test
alias:: 20 yard Shuttle, 5-10-5 Shuttle
category:: agility
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: 3 cones spaced 5 yards apart, stopwatch or timing gates  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Start straddling center cone line (three-point stance)  
2. Sprint 5 yd right, then 10 yd left, then return 5 yd to center  
3. Must touch base of each outer cone with hand  
4. Best of 2 trials recorded

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Must touch line accurately for valid rep
Change-of-direction footwork affects time greatly
Lateral lean control is key

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Gold-standard agility test for football & rugby
Male norm: ~4.5â€“4.8 sec (elite <4.5 sec)
Great for assessing deceleration & re-acceleration

    + #### 13.21 505 Agility Test
type:: test
alias:: Single-Direction Change of Direction
category:: agility
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Cones, 15m flat running surface, stopwatch or timing gates  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 timer, 1 administrator  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Set cones at start, 5m, and 15m marks  
2. Athlete sprints forward from 15m mark  
3. Time is recorded over final 10m segment (from 10m â†’ 15m and back to 10m)  
4. At 15m cone, athlete cuts 180Â°, sprints back 5m  
5. Test best of two trials per leg

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Start is moving; donâ€™t time first 10m (acceleration phase)
Use both left and right leg cuts for asymmetry screening
Clear cone contact and change of direction technique critical

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Common in rugby, soccer, court sports
Elite athletes: ~2.2â€“2.4 sec
Measures deceleration + re-acceleration ability in one direction

  + ### Speed

    + #### 13.22 Straight-Line Sprint Test
type:: test
alias:: 40 Yard Sprint
category:: speed
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Measured distance (10m/20m/40yd), timing gates or stopwatch  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1â€“2 timers or automatic timing system  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Warm up fully; perform practice starts  
2. Sprint through measured line (10m, 20m, 40yd, etc.)  
at least 2 min rest
3. Best of 2 trials  
4. Use split times if available to assess acceleration profile

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Athlete must start from same stance each trial
Avoid leaning or diving early into the line
Timing accuracy keyâ€”gates better than stopwatch

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
10m = accelerationï¼›40yd = football standard
Elite 10m: <1.8 secï¼›40m: <5.3 
Use multiple splits for sprint profiling

  + ### Balance and Stability

    + #### 13.23 Balance Error Scoring System (BESS)
type:: test
alias:: Static Postural Stability Test
category:: balance and stability
tags:: 
[:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 300
hl-color:: yellow


      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Foam pad, stopwatch, clipboard  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to count errors

      + ğŸ” Procedure:

        + 1. 3 stances Ã— 2 surfaces (firm + foam)ï¼š  
 a) Double-leg stance  
 b) Single-leg stance (non-dominant foot)  
 c) Tandem (heel-to-toe) stance (dominant foot in front of the nondominant foot)

        + 2. Each held for 20 sec, eyes closed, hands on hips

        + 3. Count errors (max 10 per condition): 
opening eyes; lifting hands from hips; touchdown of nonstance foot; step, hop, or other
lifting forefeet or heel
moving hip into more than 30Â° of hip flexion or abduction
out of position for more than 5 seconds

        + 4. Total errors across all 6 conditions = score

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Very sensitive to fatigue, concussion, inner ear issues
Inconsistent flooring or ambient noise can affect results

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Widely used in concussion screening
Lower total errors = better balance
Norms: Healthy athletes = ~10â€“15 total errors

    + #### 13.24 Star Excursion Balance Test (SEBT)
type:: test
alias:: Dynamic Balance Reach Test
category:: balance and stability
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Tape or grid to form 8-star directions (120Â° separation)  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to mark distances

        + ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Athlete stands on one leg in center  
2. Reaches opposite foot in <span class="r">3 main directions (anterior, posteromedial, posterolateral)</span> 
3. Reach as far as possible while maintaining single-leg stance 
rest at least 15 secs 
4. Distance recorded per direction; 3 trials each (take average)  
5. Normalize score to leg lengthï¼ˆreach Ã· limb length Ã— 100ï¼‰

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Loss of balance, foot tap, or shift = invalid trial
Leg length must be accurately measured (ASIS â†’ medial malleolus)
Allow practice trials for familiarization

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Effective for injury risk screeningï¼ˆesp. ankle/kneeï¼‰
Composite score <94% = â†‘ injury risk
Dynamic alternative to BESS

  + ### Flexibility

    + #### 13.25 Sit-and-Reach Test
type:: test
category:: flexibility
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Sit-and-reach box with ruler or yardstick  
24 inches(61 cm) long across the measuring stick and at a right angle to it at the 15-inch (38 cm) mark
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to monitor technique and record score  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Athlete sits, legs extended, feet flat against box, shoes off  - feet 12 inches (30 cm) apart  
2. Hands overlapped, arms extended forward  
3. Reach forward slowly and hold for 2 sec  
4. Record **best of 3 trials**

      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Jerking motion or bouncing invalidates trial
Does not isolate hamstrings (also influenced by spine, glutes)
Norms vary based on age/gender

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
One of the most common general flexibility tests
Useful for group fitness screening
Not recommended for diagnosing mobility restrictions

    + #### 13.26 Overhead Squat Test
type:: test  
category:: flexibility / movement screen  
tags::

    + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Wooden dowel or PVC pipe, open space or grid mat  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 administrator to score posture deviations  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Dowel held overhead (elbows locked), feet shoulder-width  
grip   twice shoulder-width
2. Athlete performs deep squat: hips below knee line  
3. View from front and side for compensation  
4. Grade based on quality of: torso angle, knee tracking, depth, arm position

    + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Not standardized for scoring (subjective)
Dowel mobility limits may mask true lower-body restrictions
Heels may elevate with limited ankle dorsiflexion

    + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Used in FMS (Functional Movement Screen) protocols
Good for identifying mobility + motor control deficits
Often combined with follow-up movement correctives

  + ### Body Comp

    + #### 13.27 Skinfold Measurements
type:: test
alias:: Subcutaneous Body Fat Estimation
category:: body composition
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Skinfold calipers, marker, measurement guide  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 skilled technician (training required)

      + ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Identify measurement sites (Jackson-Pollock 3- or 7-site protocols)  
 e.g., chest, abdomen, thigh (men) / triceps, suprailiac, thigh (women)  
2. Pinch skinfold perpendicular to site axis  
3. Measure to nearest 0.5 mm  
4. Perform 2 measurements/site (3 if variation >2 mm)  
5. Use formula to estimate % body fat

        + Chestâ€”a diagonal fold one-half the distance between the anterior axillary line and the nipple for men (figure 13.19a
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 304
hl-color:: yellow


        + Abdomenâ€”a vertical fold 1 inch (2.5 cm) to the right (relative to the athlete) of the umbilicus (figure 13.19c)
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 304
hl-color:: yellow


        + [:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 305
hl-color:: yellow


      + âš ï¸ Caveats:
Hydration, stress, and caliper pressure affect readings
Must be done on right side of body
Technique-sensitive; inter-rater error common

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Fast, inexpensive field estimate of body fat
Â±3.5% margin of error compared to DEXA
Useful for longitudinal tracking

  + ### Anthropometry

    + #### 13.28 Girth Measurements
type:: test
category:: anthropometry
tags::

      + ğŸ› ï¸ Equipment: Non-stretchable measuring tape  
ğŸ‘¥ Personnel: 1 trained technician, preferably same person over time  
ğŸ” Procedure:  
1. Measure girth at standard anatomical landmarks: all right  
 chest, arm, waist, hips, thigh, calf  
[:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 306
hl-color:: yellow

2. Ensure tape is horizontal, snug but not compressing skin  
3. Record to nearest 0.1 cm  
4. Use in body composition or performance profile analysis  

âš ï¸ Caveats:
Time of day, hydration, posture affect values
Must maintain consistent anatomical landmarks
Best used for trend tracking, not exact composition

      + ğŸ“Œ Notes:
Often used in police/fire/military screening
Valuable for hypertrophy or atrophy monitoring
More reliable than weight alone for body change assessment

    + ### Freq, Duration:


      + Stretching twice per week, for a minimum of five weeks, has been shown to significantly improve flexibility

      + duration: static 15-30 secs

    + ### Proprioceptors and Stretching


      + æ…¢æ…¢ä¼¸å±•ï¼šé¿å…åˆºæ¿€ muscle spindleç”¢ç”Ÿstretch reflex
ç”¢ç”Ÿtension åˆºæ¿€ Golgi Tendon Organ è®“è‚Œè‚‰æ”¾é¬†
-> same muscle being stretched : autogenic inhibition è¦æ‹‰çš„è‚Œè‚‰å…ˆä¸»å‹• contraction
-> æ”¾é¬†æ™‚ä¸»å‹•æ”¶ç¸® the opposite muscle : reciprocal inhibition

  + ## ğŸ”„ Types of Stretching


    + **Active Stretching**ï¼šå€‹é«”è‡ªèº«æ”¶ç¸®å°æŠ—

    + **Passive Stretching**ï¼šå¤–åŠ›å”åŠ©é”ROM

    + 

    + 

**Static Stretching**ï¼šç·©æ…¢ä¼¸å±•è‡³mild discomfortï¼Œç¶­æŒ15â€“30ç§’

      + å®‰å…¨æ€§é«˜ã€è‚Œè‚‰å¼µåŠ›ä½ï¼Œå¸¸ç”¨æ–¼å†·èº«ğŸ§Š

    + **Ballistic Stretching**ï¼šå‹•æ…‹å½ˆè·³æ‹‰ä¼¸ï¼Œå¯èƒ½å¼•ç™¼stretch reflexâš ï¸

    + **Dynamic Stretching**ï¼šé‹å‹•å‰ä½¿ç”¨ï¼Œä¸»å‹•æ§åˆ¶ ROMï¼Œæ¨¡æ“¬æ¯”è³½å‹•ä½œ

    + 

**PNF Stretching**ï¼ˆProprioceptive Neuromuscular Facilitationï¼‰

      + æ¶‰åŠä¸»å‹•è‚Œæ”¶ç¸®èˆ‡è¢«å‹•æ‹‰ä¼¸çµ„åˆ

      + prestretch of 10 seconds

æ¨¡å¼ï¼š

        + hold-relax - isometric contraction against force 6 secs. -> relax -> passive stretch 2nd time 30s.

        + contract-relax - ä¸åªhold, é‚„å¾€å›æ¨ concentric contraction > relax -> passive stretch 2nd time 30s.
**autogenic inhibition**

        + hold-relax with agonist contraction -> å‰é¢ä¸€æ¨£ï¼Œæœ€å¾Œ moving further into the new ROM. è‡ªå·±å‡ºåŠ›ä¼¸å±•ã€‚**reciprocal inhibition** + **autogenic inhibition**

      + Calves and ankles (figure 14.12)â€¢ Chest (figure 14.13)
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 343
hl-color:: yellow

# Ch.15 Exercise Technique for Free Weight and Machine Training

understand the general techniques involved in properly performing resistance training exercises,â€¢ provide breathing guidelines,â€¢ determine the appropriateness of wearing a weight belt,â€¢ provide recommendations for spotting free weight exercises, andâ€¢ teach proper resistance training exercise and spotting techniques.

  + ## Core Stability and Balance Training Methods


    + Core: axial skeleton and all the soft tissues with proximal attachments that originate on the axial skeleton
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 427
hl-color:: yellow



      + æ ¸å¿ƒç©©å®š = **å‹•ä½œæ•ˆç‡ + åŠ›é‡è½‰ç§»å¹³å°**

    + Isolation exercises :  dynamic or isometric muscle actions designed to isolate specific core musculature without the contribution of the lower and upper extremities


      + ex: plank, side plank

      + good for untrained and rehab. 
BUT not effective at improving sport performance

    + ### **Ground-based free weight**


      + ex: squat, deadlift, overhead press, push press

      + ç‚º **functional core training**ï¼šæ ¸å¿ƒåƒèˆ‡åŠ›å‚³å°è€Œéå–®ç¨è¨“ç·´

      + Free weight ground-based exercises offer the ideal combination of specificity and instability, especially when one is focusing on strength and power development.

  + ## Variable Resistance Training Methodsï¼ˆå¯è®Šé˜»åŠ›è¨“ç·´æ³•ï¼‰

| é¡åˆ¥ | è£ç½® | ç‰¹æ€§ |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| ğŸª éŠæ¢ï¼ˆChainsï¼‰ | free-hanging steel chains | resistance â†‘ éš¨ barbell ä¸Šå‡ |
| ğŸ§¶ å½ˆåŠ›å¸¶ï¼ˆBandsï¼‰ | looped elastic bands | å½ˆæ€§ â†‘ å¼µåŠ›ï¼Œé©åˆåŠ é€Ÿå€æ®µè² è· |

    + Constant external resistance


      + traditional resistance training methods, represents real life activities

    + Accommodating resistance (semi-isokinetic)


      + speed of movement to be controlled throughout entire ROM

      + Poor external validity, unlikely to provide adequate training stimulus

    + Variable resistance


      + Combats the changing mechanical advantage
maximize force application throughout the entire ROM

      + backsquat w/ bands - Take the other ends of the bands and place them over the ends of the bar so they rest against the outside of the weight plates on either side

hl-page:: 448
hl-color:: red

  + ## Nontraditional methods


    + ### Strongman Training


      + Tire flipping


        + tire ä¸è©²æ¯” athlete é«˜

        + çª„èƒå°é«˜å€‹æ¯”è¼ƒé›£ï¼Œå¯¬èƒå°çŸ®å€‹æ¯”è¼ƒé›£

        + Three basic techniques:


          + Sumo: When tire is raised to hip or chest height, hands are rotated to allow forward pressing action with the arms

          + Back lift: deadlift stance ended with a forward-pressing motion

          + Shoulders against the tire: Preferred technique

      + Log Lifting


        + é¡ä¼¼åš clean , midrange grip to accommodate a pronated grip position

# Ch. 17 Program Design for Resistance Training

evaluate the requirements and characteristics of a sport and assess an athlete for the purpose of designing a resistance training program;â€¢ select exercises based on type, sport specificity, technique experience, equipment availability, and time availability;â€¢ determine training frequency based on training status, sport season, load, exercise type, and other concurrent exercise;â€¢ arrange exercises in a training session according to their type;â€¢ determine 1-repetition maximum (1RM), predicted 1RM from a multiple RM, and RM loads;â€¢ assign load and repetitions based on the training goal;â€¢ know when and by how much an exercise load should be increased;â€¢ assign training volumes according to the athleteâ€™s training status and the training goal; andâ€¢ determine rest period lengths based on the training goal.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 455
hl-color:: blue


  + ## Principles

  + ### specificity


    + muscles involved, the movement pattern, and the nature of the muscle action

    + **SAID** : specific adaptation to imposed demands

    + preseason -> in-season -> postseason: generalized -> sport specific

  + ### overload : 

æ¯”ç¿’æ…£çš„å¼·åº¦å†é›£ä¸€é»é»

    + å¢åŠ  sets, æ¯é€±æ¬¡æ•¸, æ¸›å°‘ä¼‘æ¯ ç­‰ç­‰

  + ### progression
raising the number of weekly training sessions, adding more drills or exercises to each session, changing the type or technical requirements of the drills or exercises, or otherwise increasing the training stimulus.

    + | **Mechanical Work** | Force Ã— Distance | ä¸€èˆ¬ç”¨æ–¼ç‰©ç†è¨ˆç®—ï¼Œä¸å¸¸æ‡‰ç”¨æ–¼è¨“ç·´è¨­è¨ˆæœ¬èº« |

      + å¯ç”¨ %1RMã€RM æ¸¬é©—æˆ–å¤šæ¬¡RMä¼°ç®—

    + ### 1RM and Multiple-RM Testing Options

  + ## 6. Volume


    + ### å®šç¾©ï¼š

  + ## Plyometric Mechanics and Physiology


    + ### ğŸ”© Mechanical Model


      + è‚Œè‚‰è‚Œè…±å–®ä½ï¼ˆMTUï¼‰å…§çš„ **series elastic component (SEC)** å„²å­˜å½ˆæ€§èƒ½é‡ï¼Œä¸»è¦ç‚º **è‚Œè…±ï¼ˆtendonï¼‰**ï¼Œè€Œéè‚Œè‚‰æœ¬èº«

    + ### âš¡ Neurophysiological Model

    + çµåˆ SEC å½ˆæ€§å¼µåŠ› + CNS stretch reflex â†’ **â†‘ concentric power**

    + ### ğŸ” Stretch-Shortening Cycle (SSC)


      + **ä¸‰å€‹ç›¸é€£éšæ®µ**ï¼š
| Phase | å…§å®¹ | é—œéµè¦ç´  |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| 1ï¸âƒ£ **Eccentric** | è‚Œè‚‰è¢«æ‹‰é•·ã€å„²å­˜èƒ½é‡ | preloadã€è‚Œæ¢­æ´»åŒ–ã€Ia fiber |

      + âœ… **ç¸®çŸ­ amortization phase** = æå‡ SSC æ•ˆç‡ï¼ˆé€šå¸¸ < 0.2 ç§’ï¼‰

      + The stretchâ€“shortening cycle combines mechanical and neurophysiological mechanisms and is the basis of plyometric exercise. A rapid eccentric muscle action stimulates the stretch reflex and storage of elastic energy, which increase the force produced during the subsequent concentric action.

# Ch.19 Program Design and Technique for Speed and Agility Training

describe the underlying biomechanical constructs of sprint, change-of-direction, and agility performance;â€¢ apply sound movement principles to the coaching of locomotion modes and techniques;â€¢ analyze the abilities and skills needed to perform specific movement tasks;â€¢ effectively monitor the development of sprint, change-of-direction, and agility abilities;â€¢ apply sound means and methods for developing speed, change of direction, and agility; andâ€¢ design and implement training programs to maximize athletic performance.
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 537
hl-color:: blue


  + å®šç¾©


    + **Speed**ï¼šç›´ç·šåŠ é€Ÿèˆ‡æœ€å¤§é€Ÿåº¦ï¼ˆlinear sprint speedï¼‰

    + **Agility**ï¼šåŒ…æ‹¬æ”¹è®Šæ–¹å‘ï¼ˆchange-of-direction, CODï¼‰èˆ‡åæ‡‰ï¼ˆperceptual-cognitive abilitiesï¼‰

    + Speed requires the ability to accelerate and reach maximal velocity, whereas agility performance requires the use of perceptualâ€“ cognitive ability in combination with the ability to decelerate and then reaccelerate in an intended direction
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 538
hl-color:: yellow


  + ## Speed and Agility Mechanics


    + **froce**, æ˜¯ä¸€å€‹å‘é‡(vector)

    + **Speed é€Ÿç‡ æ˜¯ä¸€å€‹ç´”é‡** -> å¤šå¿« cover ä¸€æ®µè·é›¢
**Velocity é€Ÿåº¦** æ˜¯å‘é‡ ï¼ Speed + direction

    + **Rate of force development (RFD)** : max force in minimal time;  âˆ† force/ âˆ† time


      + RFD æ¯” maximum force æœ‰ç”¨ å› ç‚º
the generation of maximal contraction force takes at least 300 ms, while many sport activities consume 0 to 200 ms
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 539
hl-color:: yellow


    + **Impulse** : æ±ºå®š change of momentum çš„å¤§å°


      + = force Ã— timeï¼ˆforceâ€“time curve ä¸‹çš„é¢ç©ï¼‰

      + â†‘ impulse â†’ â†‘ momentum æ”¹è®Šï¼ˆåŠ é€Ÿã€æ¸›é€Ÿã€å†åŠ é€Ÿï¼‰

      + sprinting æ™‚ï¼Œstance phase åœ°é¢åä½œç”¨åŠ›çš„æ–½åŠ›æ™‚é–“ Ã— å¤§å°æ±ºå®š impulse

    + **braking impulse** is the production of braking forces over certain periods of time

  + ## Neurophysiological Basis for Speed


    + Nervous System


      + Strength training enhances **neural drive**, the rate and amplitude of impulses being sent from the nervous system to the target muscles
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 541
hl-color:: yellow


      + **ç¥ç¶“é©…å‹• (Neural drive)**ï¼šâ†‘ motor unit recruitment + firing rate â†’ â†‘ RFD + muscular contraction speed

    + ğŸ” SSC (Stretch-Shortening Cycle)


      + å®šç¾©ï¼šè‚Œè‚‰å…ˆè¢«å¿«é€Ÿæ‹‰é•·ï¼ˆeccentricï¼‰ï¼Œæ¥è‘—ç«‹å³çŸ­ç¸®ï¼ˆconcentricï¼‰

      +

  + ##  Running Speed - Sprinting


    + **Running speed** = stride length Ã— stride rate

    + é—œéµèƒ½åŠ›ï¼šå¿«é€Ÿæ–½åŠ›ã€çŸ­æ¥è§¸æ™‚é–“ã€æœ‰æ•ˆ SSC åˆ©ç”¨

    + Sprint speed is determined by an athleteâ€™s stride length and stride rate; more successful sprinters tend to have longer stride lengths as a result of properly directed forces into the ground while also demonstrating a more frequent stride rate. These findings suggest that RFD and proper biomechanics are two of the primary limiting factors influencing sprint performance

  + ## Program Design

    + ### è®Šé …


      + exercise interval : 1rep æ™‚é–“/è·é›¢

      + é †åº

      + é »ç‡

      + å¼·åº¦ : % max

      + Restry interval: rep é–“ / çµ„é–“ä¼‘æ¯

      + Repetition(reps)

      + Sets: Reps + rest interval

      + Series: sets+recovery interval

      + Volume: 3 sets of 5 reps

      + Work-to-rest ratio

      + Volume load: volume * intensity(weight)

# Ch.20 Program Design and Technique for Aerobic Endurance Training
  + ## Factors Related to Aerobic Endurance Performance


    + 

**VOâ‚‚max**

      + æœ€å¤§æœ‰æ°§èƒ½åŠ›ï¼Œâ†‘ training status â†’ â†‘ VOâ‚‚max

      + 

ceiling level for endurance performance

        + advance athlete é€²æ­¥å¹…åº¦æœ‰é™ï¼Œç”¨ HIIT improve peak power output, ventilatory threshold, hydrogen ion buffering, and utilization of fat as a fuel source

    + 

**Lactate Threshold (LT)**

      + %VOâ‚‚max at which [lactate]â†‘ abruptly

      + **LT** > VOâ‚‚max for predicting performance ğŸš€

      + â†‘ LT â†’ â†‘ sustainable pace

      + maximal lactate steady state = lactate ä¸å †ç©çš„é‹å‹•å¼·åº¦ï¼Œæ¯” V.O2 max or LT æ›´å¥½çš„è¡¨ç¾é æ¸¬æŒ‡æ¨™

    + 

**Exercise Economy**

      + Oâ‚‚ cost @ submax pace

      + **â†‘ Economy = åŒæ¨£ VOâ‚‚ è·‘æ›´å¿«**

      + è·Ÿé«”å‹ã€mechanicsã€neuromuscular coordination æœ‰é—œ

      + An improvement in exercise economy can enhance maximal aerobic capacity (V.O2 max) and lactate threshold

  + ## Designing an Aerobic Endurance Program


    + ### Step 1: Exercise Modeï¼ˆé‹å‹•é¡å‹ï¼‰


      + Specificityâ€¼ï¸ training è¦ mimic event demandsï¼ˆe.g. cycling vs running vs swimmingï¼‰

      + å»ºè­°ï¼š**>2x/wk** æ‰å° VO2max æœ‰å¹«åŠ©

      + â†‘ freq â†’ â†‘ adaptationsï¼Œä½†éœ€è€ƒæ…®ï¼š training status, intensity & duration & recovery, sport season

    + ### Step 3: Training Intensityï¼ˆè¨“ç·´å¼·åº¦ï¼‰


      + Most accurate: O2 æ¶ˆè€— çœ‹ % VO2max & lactate ç›£æ¸¬

      + æ±ºå®š adaptation typeï¼šLT, VOâ‚‚max, speed

      + HR monitoringï¼ˆ**Karvonen formula**ï¼‰ğŸ‘‡
**Target HR = [(æœ€é«˜HR â€“ ä¼‘æ¯HR(HRR)) Ã— %intensity] + ä¼‘æ¯HR**

      + RPE: [:span]
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 580
hl-color:: yellow


      + One **metabolic equivalent (MET)** is equal to 3.5 ml/kg/min:  O2 consumption at rest


        + 5.0 Walking, 4 miles per hour (6.4 km/h), level surface
10.5 Running, 6.7 miles per hour (10.8 km/h)

      + Power measurement - for cycling

      + | å¼·åº¦å€é–“ | è¨“ç·´ç›®çš„ |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| 60â€“70% VOâ‚‚max | LSD, recovery |
| 70â€“85% VOâ‚‚max | tempo, aerobic zone |
| >90% VOâ‚‚max | interval, HIIT |

    + ### Step 4: Exercise Durationï¼ˆæ¯æ¬¡æ™‚é–“ï¼‰


      + å–æ±ºæ–¼ï¼šå¼·åº¦ï¼ˆåæ¯”ï¼‰â†’ intensity â†‘ï¼Œduration â†“


        + goalï¼ˆrace length, base buildingï¼‰

| Training Type | å»ºè­° Duration |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |

| Tempo (85%) | 20â€“30 min |

| HIIT | 30ç§’â€“4åˆ†é˜/rep |

    + ### Step 5: Exercise Progressionï¼ˆæ¼¸é€²è¨­è¨ˆï¼‰


      + aerobic fitness does not decrease for up to five weeks when intensity of training is maintained and frequency decreases to as few as two times per week

hl-page:: 582
hl-color:: yellow


      + **å”¯ä¸€åŸå‰‡ï¼šé€æ­¥â†‘ volume OR intensity OR frequencyï¼ŒéåŒæ™‚**

| Interval | >VOâ‚‚max | 30sâ€“5min (1:1-3) | â†‘ VOâ‚‚max, tolerance |1-2|

| Fartlek | varied | 20â€“60 min | mix é©æ‡‰, mental engagement | 1 only|

    + ### 1ï¸âƒ£ Long, Slow Distance (LSD)


      + Intensityï¼š~70% VOâ‚‚max or 80% HRmaxï¼ˆRPE ~ 5ï¼‰(Zone 2)

      + Durationï¼š**é•·æ–¼æ¯”è³½è·é›¢æˆ–æ™‚é–“**

        + â†‘ aerobic base

  + ## Application of Program Design to Training Seasons

| ---- | ---- | ---- | ---|

  + ## Special Issues - cross-training, detraining, tapering, and resistance training

      + Linear taper - gradual change

  + ## Undulating Versus linear Periodization Models


    + daily undulating periodization model: large daily (i.e., within the microcycle) fluctuations in the load and volume (e.g., repetitions and volume-load) of assigned core resistance training exercises.


      + å¯èƒ½åœ¨ä¸€é€±å…§å°±æœ‰ hypertrophy, strength, power

  + Example

# Ch.22 Rehabilitation and Reconditioning

identify the members of the sports medicine team and their responsibilities during the rehabilitation and reconditioning of injured athletes,â€¢ recognize the types of injuries athletes sustain,â€¢ comprehend the timing and events of tissue healing,â€¢ understand the goals of each tissue healing phase, andâ€¢ describe the role of the strength and conditioning professional during injury rehabilitation and reconditioning.   + ### Sports Medicine team
| æˆå“¡ | è§’è‰² |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| Physician (Team doctor) | é ˜å°åœ˜éšŠã€ç¢ºå®šè¨ºæ–·èˆ‡ rehab é–‹å§‹æ™‚æ©Ÿ |

  + ### æºé€š

    + äº†è§£ rehab phase & é©åˆçš„é‹å‹•æ¨¡å¼

    + å®šæœŸæ›´æ–° progressã€pain levelã€åŠŸèƒ½ç‹€æ…‹

    + å›å ±ä»»ä½• exercise-related concernï¼ˆe.g. å‹•ä½œä»£å„Ÿã€ç–¼ç—›å›ç™¼ï¼‰

    + indication & contraindication

  + Types of injury: Macrotrauma (disloation, subluxation, sprain, contusion) vs Microtrauma

  + ### Healing Phases & Timeline

| Phase | ç‰¹å¾µ | æ™‚é–“ç¯„åœ | Focus |
| ---- | ---- | ---- | ---|
| Inflammatory | redness, swelling, pain | 0â€“4 days |â†“ pain & inflammation, passive ROM, isometrics |
| Fibroblastic Repair | collagen synthesis, scar forming | ~2 daysâ€“6 wk | begin gentle strength, active ROM, low-load isotonics |
| Maturation-Remodeling | â†‘ collagen strength, â†‘ function | â‰¥3 wkâ€“æ•¸æœˆ |â†‘ strength, neuromuscular ctrl, functional return |

  + ### Closed vs Open Kinetic Chain 

| åˆ†é¡ | ç‰¹å¾µ | ç¯„ä¾‹ |
| ---- | ---- | ---- |
| CKC | distal segment å›ºå®šï¼Œâ†‘ joint stability | squat, lunge, push-up |
| OKC | distal segment è‡ªç”±ï¼Œisolated mvmt | leg extension, biceps curl |

    + CKCï¼šâ†‘ proprioception, stability â†’ rehab early-mid stage ä½¿ç”¨

    + OKCï¼šisolated strength â†‘ â†’ ç”¨æ–¼ late stage strength rebuilding

  + ## Program design

    + Resistance Training:

      + Delorme and Oxford method:

        + 3*10 with pyramid design
Delorme - 3x10 @ 50% 10RM, 75% 10RM, and 100% 10RM respectively è¼•åˆ°é‡
Oxford - 3x10 @ 100% 10RM, 75% 10RM, 50% 10RM é‡åˆ°è¼•

### daily adjustable progressive resistive exercise (DAPRE)
![image.png](/assets/image_1751605243557_0.png)

        + 4*10 @ 50% 1RM, 6 @ 75% 1RM, max reps @ 100% estimated 1RM

    + Sport-specific movements and training structure

      + Ex: higher reps when rehabbing endurance athletes, lower for strength/power athletes

  + ### Reducing Risk of Injury and Reinjury


    + Previous injury is one of the most substantial risk factors for additional injury

    + Risk factors for upper extremity injury : â†“  shoulder ROM, scapular dyskinesis, and â†“  shoulder strength


      + include ROM exercises and possibly the â€œThrowerâ€™s Tenâ€

    + Risk factors for lower extremity injury - â†“  balance,  neuromuscular control during jump landing, and lower extremity strength


      + single leg squat, plyometric landings

  + ## ğŸ¯ High Yield / Take-Home Points

    + Rehab = team workï¼Œ**S&C â‰  rehab leaderï¼Œä½†æ˜¯é—œéµå¯¦è¸è€…**

    + Rehab phase æ˜ç¢ºå€åˆ† â†’ phase-specific training ğŸ“ˆ

    + Healing â‰  linear progressionï¼Œé ˆéš¨æ™‚èª¿æ•´

    + **æºé€šæµæš¢ = æœ€é‡è¦çš„ injury recovery é æ¸¬å› å­ä¹‹ä¸€**

weight plate: 18 inches wide

  + ## Arranging equipment


    + Barbells and dumbbells should have a minimum of36 inches (91 cm) of space between other barbells and dumbbells to allow for movement between racks without danger to the lifter or spotter(s). 
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 645
hl-color:: yellow


    + walkways at least 36in wide

    + at least 49 square feet (4.6 m2) of open space so that athletes can perform a dynamic warm-up 
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 645
hl-color:: yellow


    + Circuit training


      + circuit training machines should be at least 24 inches (61 cm)â€”preferably 36 inches (91 cm)â€”away from each other in order to provide a wide enough walk space and some extra space for safety called a safety cushion. 
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 645
hl-color:: yellow


      + Any designated walkways in the circuit training area should be between 4 and 7 feet (1.2 and 2.1 m) wide
ls-type:: annotation
hl-page:: 645
hl-color:: yellow


    + Racks and platforms should be spaced 3 to 4 feet (0.9 to 1.2 m) apart.


      + weightlifting racks should be bolted to the floor
